data-file-width="300" data-file-height="300" /> \xc2\xa31,473 <a href="/wiki/1,000,000_(number)" class="mw-redirect" title="1,000,000 (number)">million</a> (2016)<sup id="cite_ref-5" href="http://www.britishairways.com">www<wbr />.britishairways<wbr />.com</a></span></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>British Airways</b> (<b>BA</b>) is the <a href="/wiki/Flag_carrier" title="Flag carrier">flag carrier</a><sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> and the largest airline in the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a> based on fleet size, or the second largest, behind <a href="/wiki/EasyJet" title="EasyJet">easyJet</a>, when measured by passengers carried. The airline is based in <a href="/wiki/Waterside_(building)" title="Waterside (building)">Waterside</a> near its main <a href="/wiki/Airline_hub" title="Airline hub">hub</a> at <a href="/wiki/London_Heathrow_Airport" class="mw-redirect" title="London Heathrow Airport">London Heathrow Airport</a>. In January 2011 BA merged with <a href="/wiki/Iberia_(airline)" title="Iberia (airline)">Iberia</a>, creating the <a href="/wiki/International_Airlines_Group" title="International Airlines Group">International Airlines Group</a> (IAG), a <a href="/wiki/Holding_company" title="Holding company">holding company</a> registered in <a href="/wiki/Madrid" title="Madrid">Madrid</a>, Spain. IAG is the world\'s third-largest airline group in terms of annual revenue and the second-largest in Europe. It is listed on the <a href="/wiki/London_Stock_Exchange" title="London Stock Exchange">London Stock Exchange</a> and in the <a href="/wiki/FTSE_100_Index" title="FTSE 100 Index">FTSE 100 Index</a>.\n</p><p>BA was created in 1974 after a British Airways Board was established by the British government to manage the two nationalised airline corporations, <a href="/wiki/British_Overseas_Airways_Corporation" title="British Overseas Airways Corporation">British Overseas Airways Corporation</a> and <a href="/wiki/British_European_Airways" title="British European Airways">British European Airways</a>, and two regional airlines, <a href="/wiki/Cambrian_Airways" title="Cambrian Airways">Cambrian Airways</a> from <a href="/wiki/Cardiff" title="Cardiff">Cardiff</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Northeast_Airlines_(UK)" title="Northeast Airlines (UK)">Northeast Airlines</a> from <a href="/wiki/Newcastle_upon_Tyne" title="Newcastle upon Tyne">Newcastle upon Tyne</a>. On 31 March 1974, all four companies were merged to form British Airways. After almost 13 years as a state company, BA was privatised in February 1987 as part of a wider privatisation plan by the <a href="/wiki/Second_Thatcher_ministry" title="Second Thatcher ministry">Conservative government</a>. The carrier expanded with the acquisition of <a href="/wiki/British_Caledonian" title="British Caledonian">British Caledonian</a> in 1987, <a href="/wiki/Dan-Air" title="Dan-Air">Dan-Air</a> in 1992, and <a href="/wiki/British_Midland_International" title="British Midland International">British Midland International</a> in 2012.\n</p><p>A long-time <a href="/wiki/Boeing" title="Boeing">Boeing</a> customer, BA ordered 59 <a href="/wiki/Airbus_A320_family" title="Airbus A320 family">Airbus A320 family</a> aircraft in August 1998. In 2007 it purchased 12 <a href="/wiki/Airbus_A380" title="Airbus A380">Airbus A380s</a> and 24 <a href="/wiki/Boeing_787_Dreamliner" title="Boeing 787 Dreamliner">Boeing 787 Dreamliners</a>, marking the start of its <a href="/wiki/Flight_length" title="Flight length">long-haul</a> fleet replacement. The centrepiece of the airline\'s long-haul fleet is the <a href="/wiki/Boeing_777" title="Boeing 777">Boeing 777</a>, with 58 in the fleet. BA is the largest operator of the <a href="/wiki/Boeing_747-400" title="Boeing 747-400">Boeing 747-400</a>, with 41 registered to the airline. It is a founding member of the <a href="/wiki/Oneworld" title="Oneworld">Oneworld</a> airline alliance, along with <a href="/wiki/American_Airlines" title="American Airlines">American Airlines</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cathay_Pacific" title="Cathay Pacific">Cathay Pacific</a>, <a href="/wiki/Qantas" title="Qantas">Qantas</a>, and the now defunct <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Airlines" title="Canadian Airlines">Canadian Airlines</a>. The alliance has since grown to become the third largest, after <a href="/wiki/SkyTeam" title="SkyTeam">SkyTeam</a> and <a href="/wiki/Star_Alliance" title="Star Alliance">Star Alliance</a>.\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" class="toclevel-2 tocsection-17"><a href="#Liveries_and_tail_fins"><span class="tocnumber">5.2</span> <span class="toctext">Liveries and tail fins</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-18"><a href="#Loyalty_programmes"><span class="tocnumber">5.3</span> <span class="toctext">Loyalty programmes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-19"><a href="#Inflight_magazines"><span class="tocnumber">5.4</span> <span class="toctext">Inflight magazines</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-20"><a href="#Cabins_and_services"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Cabins and services</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-21"><a href="#Short_haul"><span class="tocnumber">6.1</span> <span class="toctext">Short haul</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-22"><a href="#Economy_class"><span class="tocnumber">6.1.1</span> <span class="toctext">Economy class</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-23"><a href="#Business_class"><span class="tocnumber">6.1.2</span> <span class="toctext">Business class</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-24"><a href="#Mid-haul_and_Long_haul"><span class="tocnumber">6.2</span> <span class="toctext">Mid-haul and Long haul</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-25"><a href="#First_class"><span class="tocnumber">6.2.1</span> <span class="toctext">First class</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-26"><a href="#Club_World"><span class="tocnumber">6.2.2</span> <span class="toctext">Club World</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-27"><a href="#World_Traveller_Plus"><span class="tocnumber">6.2.3</span> <span class="toctext">World Traveller Plus</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-28"><a href="#World_Traveller"><span class="tocnumber">6.2.4</span> <span class="toctext">World Traveller</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-29"><a href="#Incidents_and_accidents"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Incidents and accidents</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-30"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-31"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-32"><a href="#Bibliography"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">Bibliography</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-33"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_British_Airways" title="History of British Airways">History of British Airways</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:British_Airways_Boeing_747-100_in_BOAC_basic_livery_Marmet.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/British_Airways_Boeing_747-100_in_BOAC_basic_livery_Marmet.jpg/220px-British_Airways_Boeing_747-100_in_BOAC_basic_livery_Marmet.jpg" width="220" height="144" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/British_Airways_Boeing_747-100_in_BOAC_basic_livery_Marmet.jpg/330px-British_Airways_Boeing_747-100_in_BOAC_basic_livery_Marmet.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/British_Airways_Boeing_747-100_in_BOAC_basic_livery_Marmet.jpg/440px-British_Airways_Boeing_747-100_in_BOAC_basic_livery_Marmet.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="672" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:British_Airways_Boeing_747-100_in_BOAC_basic_livery_Marmet.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Boeing_747-100" class="mw-redirect" title="Boeing 747-100">Boeing 747-100</a> in BOAC-British Airways transition livery</div></div></div>\n<p>Proposals to establish a joint British airline, combining the assets of the <a href="/wiki/British_Overseas_Airways_Corporation" title="British Overseas Airways Corporation">British Overseas Airways Corporation</a> (BOAC) and <a href="/wiki/British_European_Airways" title="British European Airways">British European Airways</a> (BEA) were first raised in 1953 as a result of difficulties in attempts by BOAC and BEA to negotiate air rights through the British colony of <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a>. Increasingly BOAC was protesting that BEA was using its subsidiary <a href="/wiki/Cyprus_Airways" class="mw-redirect" title="Cyprus Airways">Cyprus Airways</a> to circumvent an agreement that BEA would not fly routes further east than Cyprus, particularly to the increasingly important oil regions in the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a>. The Chairman of BOAC, Miles Thomas, was in favour of merger as a potential solution to this disagreement and had backing for the idea from the <a href="/wiki/Chancellor_of_the_Exchequer" title="Chancellor of the Exchequer">Chancellor of the Exchequer</a> at the time, <a href="/wiki/Rab_Butler" title="Rab Butler">Rab Butler</a>. However, opposition from the Treasury blocked the proposal.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Consequently, it was only following the recommendations of the 1969 Edwards Report that a new British Airways Board, managing both BEA and BOAC, and the two regional British airlines <a href="/wiki/Cambrian_Airways" title="Cambrian Airways">Cambrian Airways</a> based at Cardiff, and <a href="/wiki/Northeast_Airlines_(UK)" title="Northeast Airlines (UK)">Northeast Airlines</a> based at Newcastle upon Tyne, was constituted on 1 April 1972.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Although each airline\'s individual branding was maintained initially, two years later the British Airways Board unified its branding, effectively establishing British Airways as an airline on 31 March 1974.<sup id="cite_ref-EOP7_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EOP7-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Following two years of fierce competition with <a href="/wiki/British_Caledonian" title="British Caledonian">British Caledonian</a>, the second-largest airline in the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a> at the time, the Government changed its aviation policy in 1976 so that the two carriers would no longer compete on long-haul routes.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>British Airways and <a href="/wiki/Air_France" title="Air France">Air France</a> operated the <a href="/wiki/Supersonic" class="mw-redirect" title="Supersonic">supersonic</a> airliner <a href="/wiki/Concorde" title="Concorde">Aerospatiale-BAC Concorde</a>, and the world\'s first <a href="/wiki/Supersonic" class="mw-redirect" title="Supersonic">supersonic</a> passenger service flew in January 1976 from <a href="/wiki/London_Heathrow" class="mw-redirect" title="London Heathrow">London Heathrow</a> to <a href="/wiki/Bahrain" title="Bahrain">Bahrain</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Concordeintro3_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Concordeintro3-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Services to the US began on 24 May 1976 with a flight to <a href="/wiki/Washington_Dulles_International_Airport" title="Washington Dulles International Airport">Washington Dulles</a> airport, and flights to <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy_International_Airport" title="John F. Kennedy International Airport">New York JFK</a> airport followed on 22 September 1977. Service to Singapore was established in co-operation with <a href="/wiki/Singapore_Airlines" title="Singapore Airlines">Singapore Airlines</a> as a continuation of the flight to Bahrain.<sup id="cite_ref-EOP7_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EOP7-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Following the <a href="/wiki/Air_France_Flight_4590" title="Air France Flight 4590">Air France Concorde crash</a> in Paris and a slump in air travel following the <a href="/wiki/11_September_attacks" class="mw-redirect" title="11 September attacks">11 September attacks</a> in New York in 2001, it was decided to cease Concorde operations in 2003 after 27 years of service. The final commercial Concorde flight was BA002 from New York JFK to London Heathrow on 24 October 2003.<sup id="cite_ref-EOP2000_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EOP2000-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:British_Airways_Trident_Three_G-AWZA.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/British_Airways_Trident_Three_G-AWZA.jpg/220px-British_Airways_Trident_Three_G-AWZA.jpg" width="220" height="114" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/British_Airways_Trident_Three_G-AWZA.jpg/330px-British_Airways_Trident_Three_G-AWZA.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/British_Airways_Trident_Three_G-AWZA.jpg/440px-British_Airways_Trident_Three_G-AWZA.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2041" data-file-height="1061" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:British_Airways_Trident_Three_G-AWZA.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A British Airways <a href="/wiki/Hawker_Siddeley_Trident" title="Hawker Siddeley Trident">Hawker Siddeley Trident</a> in transitional scheme with BEA livery but with British Airways titles</div></div></div>\n<p>In 1981 the airline was instructed to prepare for privatisation by the <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Conservative Party (UK)">Conservative</a> <a href="/wiki/Second_Thatcher_ministry" title="Second Thatcher ministry">Thatcher</a> government. <a href="/wiki/John_King,_Baron_King_of_Wartnaby" title="John King, Baron King of Wartnaby">Sir John King</a>, later Lord King, was appointed chairman, charged with bringing the airline back into profitability. While many other large airlines struggled, King was credited with transforming British Airways into one of the most profitable air carriers in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> The flag carrier was privatised and was floated on the London Stock Exchange in February 1987.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> British Airways effected the takeover of the UK\'s "second" airline, British Caledonian, in July of that same year.<sup id="cite_ref-BAP_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BAP-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The formation of <a href="/wiki/Richard_Branson" title="Richard Branson">Richard Branson</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Virgin_Atlantic" title="Virgin Atlantic">Virgin Atlantic</a> in 1984 created a competitor for BA. The intense rivalry between British Airways and Virgin Atlantic culminated in the former being sued for libel in 1993, arising from claims and counterclaims over a "dirty tricks" campaign against Virgin. This campaign included allegations of poaching Virgin Atlantic customers, tampering with private files belonging to Virgin and undermining Virgin\'s reputation in the City. As a result of the case BA management apologised "unreservedly", and the company agreed to pay \xc2\xa3110,000 damages to Virgin, \xc2\xa3500,000 to Branson personally and \xc2\xa33&#160;million legal costs.<sup id="cite_ref-dirtytricks_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dirtytricks-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> Lord King stepped down as chairman in 1993 and was replaced by his deputy, Colin Marshall, while <a href="/wiki/Robert_Ayling" title="Robert Ayling">Bob Ayling</a> took over as CEO.<sup id="cite_ref-EOP9_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EOP9-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> Virgin filed a separate action in the US that same year regarding BA\'s domination of the trans-Atlantic routes, but it was thrown out in 1999.<sup id="cite_ref-dirtytricks_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dirtytricks-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:British_Airways_Concorde_official_handover_ceremony_Fitzgerald.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/British_Airways_Concorde_official_handover_ceremony_Fitzgerald.jpg/220px-British_Airways_Concorde_official_handover_ceremony_Fitzgerald.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/British_Airways_Concorde_official_handover_ceremony_Fitzgerald.jpg/330px-British_Airways_Concorde_official_handover_ceremony_Fitzgerald.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/British_Airways_Concorde_official_handover_ceremony_Fitzgerald.jpg/440px-British_Airways_Concorde_official_handover_ceremony_Fitzgerald.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1023" data-file-height="681" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:British_Airways_Concorde_official_handover_ceremony_Fitzgerald.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>British Airways\' first <a href="/wiki/Concorde" title="Concorde">Concorde</a> at Heathrow Airport, on 15 January 1976</div></div></div>\n<p>In 1992 British Airways expanded through the acquisition of the financially troubled <a href="/wiki/Dan-Air" title="Dan-Air">Dan-Air</a>, giving BA a much larger presence at Gatwick airport. <a href="/wiki/British_Asia_Airways" title="British Asia Airways">British Asia Airways</a>, a subsidiary based in Taiwan, was formed in March 1993 to operate between London and <a href="/wiki/Taipei" title="Taipei">Taipei</a>. That same month BA purchased a 25% stake in the Australian airline <a href="/wiki/Qantas" title="Qantas">Qantas</a> and, with the acquisition of <a href="/wiki/Brymon_Airways" title="Brymon Airways">Brymon Airways</a> in May, formed British Airways Citiexpress (later <a href="/wiki/BA_Connect" title="BA Connect">BA Connect</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-EOP9_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EOP9-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> In September 1998, British Airways, along with <a href="/wiki/American_Airlines" title="American Airlines">American Airlines</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cathay_Pacific" title="Cathay Pacific">Cathay Pacific</a>, Qantas, and <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Airlines" title="Canadian Airlines">Canadian Airlines</a>, formed the <a href="/wiki/Oneworld" title="Oneworld">Oneworld</a> <a href="/wiki/Airline_alliance" title="Airline alliance">airline alliance</a>. Oneworld began operations on 1 February 1999, and is the third largest airline alliance in the world, behind <a href="/wiki/SkyTeam" title="SkyTeam">SkyTeam</a> and Star Alliance.<sup id="cite_ref-BAP_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BAP-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Bob Ayling\'s leadership led to a cost savings of \xc2\xa3750m and the establishment of a budget airline, <a href="/wiki/Go_(airline)" title="Go (airline)">Go</a>, in 1998.<sup id="cite_ref-aylingquits_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aylingquits-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> The next year, however, British Airways reported an 84% drop in profits in its first quarter alone, its worst in seven years.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> In March 2000, Ayling was removed from his position and British Airways announced <a href="/wiki/Rod_Eddington" title="Rod Eddington">Rod Eddington</a> as his successor. That year, British Airways and <a href="/wiki/KLM" title="KLM">KLM</a> conducted talks on a potential merger, reaching a decision in July to file an official merger plan with the European Commission.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> The plan fell through in September 2000.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> British Asia Airways ceased operations in 2001 after BA suspended flights to Taipei. Go was sold to its management and the private equity firm <a href="/wiki/3i" title="3i">3i</a> in June 2001.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Eddington would make further workforce cuts due to reduced demand following 11 September attacks in 2001,<sup id="cite_ref-EOP2000_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EOP2000-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> and BA sold its stake in Qantas in September 2004.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> In 2005 <a href="/wiki/Willie_Walsh_(Irish_businessman)" class="mw-redirect" title="Willie Walsh (Irish businessman)">Willie Walsh</a>, managing director of <a href="/wiki/Aer_Lingus" title="Aer Lingus">Aer Lingus</a> and a former pilot, became the chief executive officer of British Airways.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> BA unveiled its new subsidiary <a href="/wiki/OpenSkies" title="OpenSkies">OpenSkies</a> in January 2008, taking advantage of the liberalisation of transatlantic traffic rights between Europe and the United States. OpenSkies flies non-stop from Paris to New York\'s JFK and Newark airports.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:British_Airways_Iberia_aircraft_tails_BA_IB.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/British_Airways_Iberia_aircraft_tails_BA_IB.jpg/220px-British_Airways_Iberia_aircraft_tails_BA_IB.jpg" width="220" height="124" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/British_Airways_Iberia_aircraft_tails_BA_IB.jpg/330px-British_Airways_Iberia_aircraft_tails_BA_IB.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/British_Airways_Iberia_aircraft_tails_BA_IB.jpg/440px-British_Airways_Iberia_aircraft_tails_BA_IB.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="451" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:British_Airways_Iberia_aircraft_tails_BA_IB.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>British Airways and Iberia merged in January 2011, forming the International Airlines Group, one of the world\'s largest airlines</div></div></div>\n<p>On July 2008 British Airways announced a merger plan with <a href="/wiki/Iberia_(airline)" title="Iberia (airline)">Iberia</a>, another flag carrier airline in the Oneworld alliance, wherein each airline would retain its original brand.<sup id="cite_ref-baiberiamerger_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-baiberiamerger-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> The agreement was confirmed in April 2010,<sup id="cite_ref-BAIM_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BAIM-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> and in July the <a href="/wiki/European_Commission" title="European Commission">European Commission</a> and <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Transportation" title="United States Department of Transportation">US Department of Transport</a> permitted the merger and began to co-ordinate <a href="/wiki/Transatlantic_flight" title="Transatlantic flight">transatlantic</a> routes with American Airlines.<sup id="cite_ref-ECABAIB_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ECABAIB-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-DAOAIA_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DAOAIA-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> On 6 October 2010 the alliance between British Airways, American Airlines and Iberia formally began operations. The alliance generates an estimated \xc2\xa3230&#160;million in annual cost-saving for BA, in addition to the \xc2\xa3330&#160;million which would be saved by the merger with Iberia.<sup id="cite_ref-BAIAAS_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BAIAAS-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> This merger was finalised on 21 January 2011, resulting in the International Airlines Group (IAG), the world\'s third-largest airline in terms of annual revenue and the second-largest airline group in Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-BAIM_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BAIM-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> Prior to merging, British Airways owned a 13.5% stake in Iberia, and thus received ownership of 55% of the combined International Airlines Group; Iberia\'s other shareholders received the remaining 45%.<sup id="cite_ref-TEP_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TEP-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> As a part of the merger, British Airways ceased trading independently on the <a href="/wiki/London_Stock_Exchange" title="London Stock Exchange">London Stock Exchange</a> after 23 years as a constituent of the <a href="/wiki/FTSE_100_Index" title="FTSE 100 Index">FTSE 100 Index</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In September 2010 Willie Walsh, now CEO of IAG, announced that the group was considering acquiring other airlines and had drawn up a shortlist of twelve possible acquisitions.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> In November 2011 IAG announced an agreement in principle to purchase British Midland International from <a href="/wiki/Lufthansa" title="Lufthansa">Lufthansa</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> A contract to purchase the airline was agreed the next month,<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> and the sale was completed for \xc2\xa3172.5 million on 30 March 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> The airline established a new subsidiary based at <a href="/wiki/London_City_Airport" title="London City Airport">London City Airport</a> operating <a href="/wiki/Airbus_A318" title="Airbus A318">Airbus A318s</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>British Airways was the official airline partner of the <a href="/wiki/2012_Summer_Olympics" title="2012 Summer Olympics">London 2012 Olympic Games</a>. On 18 May 2012 it flew the Olympic flame from <a href="/wiki/Athens_International_Airport" title="Athens International Airport">Athens International Airport</a> to <a href="/wiki/RNAS_Culdrose_(HMS_Seahawk)" title="RNAS Culdrose (HMS Seahawk)">RNAS Culdrose</a> while carrying various dignitaries, including Lord <a href="/wiki/Sebastian_Coe" title="Sebastian Coe">Sebastian Coe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Princess_Anne" class="mw-redirect" title="Princess Anne">Princess Anne</a>, the Olympics minister <a href="/wiki/Hugh_Robertson_(politician)" title="Hugh Robertson (politician)">Hugh Robertson</a> and the London Mayor <a href="/wiki/Boris_Johnson" title="Boris Johnson">Boris Johnson</a>, along with the footballer <a href="/wiki/David_Beckham" title="David Beckham">David Beckham</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 27 May 2017, British Airways suffered a computer power failure. All flights were cancelled and thousands of passengers were affected.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> By the following day, the company had not succeeded in reestablishing normal function of their computer systems. When asked by reporters for more information on the ongoing problems, British Airways stated "The root cause was a power supply issue which our affected our IT systems - we continue to investigate this" and declined to comment further.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Willie_Walsh_(businessman)" title="Willie Walsh (businessman)">Willie Walsh</a> later attributed the crash to an electrical engineer disconnecting the <a href="/wiki/Uninterruptible_power_supply" title="Uninterruptible power supply">UPS</a> and said there would be an independent investigation.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The decline in the value of Iranian currency due to the reintroduction of US sanctions BA has announced that the Iranian route is "not commercially viable". As a result, BA decided to stop their services in Iran, effective 22 September 2018.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Corporate_affairs">Corporate affairs</span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Operations">Operations</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/List_of_British_Airways_destinations" title="List of British Airways destinations">List of British Airways destinations</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_British_Airways_franchise_destinations" title="List of British Airways franchise destinations">List of British Airways franchise destinations</a></div>\n<p>British Airways is the largest airline based in the United Kingdom in terms of fleet size, international flights, and international destinations and was, until 2008, the largest airline by passenger numbers. The airline carried 34.6&#160;million passengers in 2008, but, rival carrier <a href="/wiki/EasyJet" title="EasyJet">easyJet</a> transported 44.5&#160;million passengers that year, passing British Airways for the first time.<sup id="cite_ref-British_Airways_Traffic_Statistics_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-British_Airways_Traffic_Statistics-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Passenger_statistics_for_December_2008_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Passenger_statistics_for_December_2008-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> British Airways holds a <a href="/wiki/Civil_Aviation_Authority_(United_Kingdom)" title="Civil Aviation Authority (United Kingdom)">United Kingdom Civil Aviation Authority</a> Type A Operating Licence, it is permitted to carry passengers, cargo, and mail on aircraft with 20 or more seats.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:WatersideBAHQLondon.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/WatersideBAHQLondon.jpg/170px-WatersideBAHQLondon.jpg" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/WatersideBAHQLondon.jpg/255px-WatersideBAHQLondon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/WatersideBAHQLondon.jpg/340px-WatersideBAHQLondon.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2370" data-file-height="3160" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:WatersideBAHQLondon.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Waterside_(building)" title="Waterside (building)">Waterside</a>, the head office of British Airways.</div></div></div>\n<p>The airlines\' head office, <a href="/wiki/Waterside_(building)" title="Waterside (building)">Waterside</a>, stands in <a href="/wiki/Harmondsworth" title="Harmondsworth">Harmondsworth</a>, a village that is near London Heathrow Airport.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> Waterside was completed in June 1998 to replace British Airways\' previous head office, Speedbird House, which was located on the grounds of Heathrow.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>British Airways\' main base is at London Heathrow Airport, but it also has a major presence at <a href="/wiki/Gatwick_Airport" title="Gatwick Airport">Gatwick Airport</a>. It also has a base at <a href="/wiki/London_City_Airport" title="London City Airport">London City Airport</a> (LCY), where its subsidiary <a href="/wiki/BA_CityFlyer" title="BA CityFlyer">BA CityFlyer</a> is the largest operator. BA had previously operated a significant hub at <a href="/wiki/Manchester_Airport" title="Manchester Airport">Manchester Airport</a>. Manchester to New York (JFK) services were withdrawn; later all international services outside London ceased when the subsidiary <a href="/wiki/BA_Connect" title="BA Connect">BA Connect</a> was sold. Passengers wishing to travel internationally with BA either to or from regional UK destinations must now transfer in London.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> Heathrow Airport is dominated by British Airways, which owns 40% of the slots available at the airport.<sup id="cite_ref-slotsref_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-slotsref-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> The majority of BA services operate from <a href="/wiki/London_Heathrow_Terminal_5" class="mw-redirect" title="London Heathrow Terminal 5">Terminal 5</a>, with the exception of some short-haul and mid-haul flights at <a href="/wiki/London_Heathrow_Terminal_1" class="mw-redirect" title="London Heathrow Terminal 1">Terminal 1</a> arising from the purchase of BMI and some short-haul flights at <a href="/wiki/London_Heathrow_Terminal_3" class="mw-redirect" title="London Heathrow Terminal 3">Terminal 3</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> owing to a lack of capacity at Terminal 5. With the imminent opening of the brand-new Terminal 2 in 2014, Star Alliance airlines will progressively be moving all their services into the new terminal and Terminal 1 will be closed for demolition in due course. British Airways\' services will then be concentrated in Terminals 3 and 5.\n</p><p>In August 2014, Willie Walsh advised the airline would continue to use flight paths over Iraq despite the hostilities there. A few days earlier Qantas announced it would avoid Iraqi airspace, while other airlines did likewise. The issue arose following the downing of <a href="/wiki/Malaysia_Airlines_Flight_17" title="Malaysia Airlines Flight 17">Malaysia Airlines Flight 17</a> over Ukraine, and a temporary suspension of flights to and from <a href="/wiki/Ben_Gurion_Airport" title="Ben Gurion Airport">Ben Gurion Airport</a> during the <a href="/wiki/2014_Israel%E2%80%93Gaza_conflict" title="2014 Israel\xe2\x80\x93Gaza conflict">2014 Israel\xe2\x80\x93Gaza conflict</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-IraqBA_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IraqBA-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Subsidiaries_and_shareholdings">Subsidiaries and shareholdings</span></h3>\n<p><a href="/wiki/BA_CityFlyer" title="BA CityFlyer">BA CityFlyer</a>, a wholly owned subsidiary, offers flights from its base at London City Airport to 23 destinations throughout Europe. It flies 17 <a href="/wiki/Embraer" title="Embraer">Embraer</a> E-170/E-190 aircraft and two leased <a href="/wiki/Saab_2000" title="Saab 2000">Saab 2000</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> The airline focuses on serving the financial market, though it has recently expanded into the leisure market, offering routes to Ibiza, Palma and Venice.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> The onboard product is identical to that of the BA Short Haul product from both LHR and LGW.<sup id="cite_ref-cityref_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cityref-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In March 2015, <a href="/wiki/Qatar_Airways" title="Qatar Airways">Qatar Airways</a> purchased a 10% stake in <a href="/wiki/International_Airlines_Group" title="International Airlines Group">International Airlines Group</a>, the parent of British Airways and <a href="/wiki/Iberia_(airline)" title="Iberia (airline)">Iberia</a>, for \xe2\x82\xac1.2 billion (US$1.26 billion).<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>BEA Helicopters was renamed <a href="/wiki/British_Airways_Helicopters" title="British Airways Helicopters">British Airways Helicopters</a> in 1974 and operated passenger and offshore oil support services until it was sold in 1986.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> Other former subsidiaries include the German airline <a href="/wiki/Deutsche_BA" class="mw-redirect" title="Deutsche BA">Deutsche BA</a> from 1997 until 2003 and the French airline <a href="/wiki/Air_Libert%C3%A9" title="Air Libert\xc3\xa9">Air Libert\xc3\xa9</a> from 1997 to 2001.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> British Airways also owned Airways Aero Association, the operator of the <a href="/wiki/Airways_Flying_Club" title="Airways Flying Club">British Airways flying club</a> based at <a href="/wiki/Wycombe_Air_Park" title="Wycombe Air Park">Wycombe Air Park</a> in <a href="/wiki/High_Wycombe" title="High Wycombe">High Wycombe</a>, until it was sold to <a href="/wiki/Surinder_Arora" title="Surinder Arora">Surinder Arora</a> in 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>South Africa\'s <a href="/wiki/Comair_(South_Africa)" title="Comair (South Africa)">Comair</a> and Denmark\'s <a href="/wiki/Sun_Air_of_Scandinavia" class="mw-redirect" title="Sun Air of Scandinavia">Sun Air of Scandinavia</a> have been <a href="/wiki/Franchisee" class="mw-redirect" title="Franchisee">franchisees</a> of British Airways since 1996.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> British Airways obtained a 15% stake in UK regional airline <a href="/wiki/Flybe" title="Flybe">Flybe</a> from the sale of BA Connect in March 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> It sold the stake in 2014. BA also owned a 10% stake in InterCapital and Regional Rail (ICRR), the company that managed the operations of <a href="/wiki/Eurostar_International_Limited" title="Eurostar International Limited">Eurostar (UK) Ltd</a> from 1998 to 2010,<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> when the management of Eurostar was restructured.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:2010-06-30_B757_OpenSkies_F-HAVN_EDDF_02.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/2010-06-30_B757_OpenSkies_F-HAVN_EDDF_02.jpg/220px-2010-06-30_B757_OpenSkies_F-HAVN_EDDF_02.jpg" width="220" height="143" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/2010-06-30_B757_OpenSkies_F-HAVN_EDDF_02.jpg/330px-2010-06-30_B757_OpenSkies_F-HAVN_EDDF_02.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/2010-06-30_B757_OpenSkies_F-HAVN_EDDF_02.jpg/440px-2010-06-30_B757_OpenSkies_F-HAVN_EDDF_02.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3087" data-file-height="2000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:2010-06-30_B757_OpenSkies_F-HAVN_EDDF_02.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>OpenSkies Boeing 757 landing at Frankfurt Airport</div></div></div>\n<p>With the creation of an <a href="/wiki/EU%E2%80%93US_Open_Skies_Agreement" title="EU\xe2\x80\x93US Open Skies Agreement">Open Skies agreement</a> between Europe and the United States in March 2008, British Airways started a new subsidiary airline called <a href="/wiki/OpenSkies" title="OpenSkies">OpenSkies</a> (previously known as \xe2\x80\x9cProject Lauren\xe2\x80\x9d).<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> The airline started operations in June 2008, and flew directly from Paris\xe2\x80\x94Orly to Newark.<sup id="cite_ref-os13_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-os13-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> However it ceased operations on September 2, 2018 when it was replaced with <a href="/wiki/Level_(airline)" title="Level (airline)">Level</a> flights on that route.<sup id="cite_ref-iagofficial_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iagofficial-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/British_Airways_Ltd_(2012%E2%80%9315)" title="British Airways Ltd (2012\xe2\x80\x9315)">British Airways Limited</a> was established in 2012 to take over the operation of the premium service between London City Airport and New York-JFK. BA began the service in September 2009, using two Airbus A318s fitted with 32 lie-flat beds in an all business class cabin.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> Flights operate under the numbers previously reserved for Concorde: BA001 \xe2\x80\x94 BA004.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> The flights returned to be directly operated by British Airways plc in 2015.\n</p><p>British Airways provides cargo services under the <a href="/wiki/British_Airways_World_Cargo" title="British Airways World Cargo">British Airways World Cargo</a> brand. The division has been part of <a href="/wiki/IAG_Cargo" title="IAG Cargo">IAG Cargo</a> since 2012, and is the world\'s twelfth-largest <a href="/wiki/Cargo_airline" title="Cargo airline">cargo airline</a> based on total freight tonne-kilometres flown.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> BA World Cargo operates using the main BA fleet. Until the end of March 2014 they also operated three <a href="/wiki/Boeing_747-8" title="Boeing 747-8">Boeing 747-8</a> freighter aircraft providing dedicated long-haul services under a <a href="/wiki/Wet_lease" class="mw-redirect" title="Wet lease">wet lease</a> arrangement from <a href="/wiki/Global_Supply_Systems" title="Global Supply Systems">Global Supply Systems</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> The division operates an automated cargo centre at London Heathrow Airport and handles freight at Gatwick and Stansted airports.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Business_trends">Business trends</span></h3>\n<p>The key trends for the British Airways Plc Group are shown below.\n</p><p>On the merger with Iberia, the accounting reference date was changed from 31 March to 31 December; figures below are therefore for the years to 31 March up to 2010, for the nine months to 31 December 2010, and for the years to href="#cite_note-Report2015-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>\n</td>\n<td style="text-align:center;"><sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>\n</td>\n<td style="text-align:center;"><sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Industrial_relations">Industrial relations</span></h3>\n<p>Staff working for British Airways are represented by a number of trade unions, pilots are represented by <a href="/wiki/British_Air_Line_Pilots%27_Association" class="mw-redirect" title="British Air Line Pilots&#39; Association">British Air Line Pilots\' Association</a>, cabin crew by <a href="/wiki/British_Airlines_Stewards_and_Stewardesses_Association" title="British Airlines Stewards and Stewardesses Association">British Airlines Stewards and Stewardesses Association</a> (a branch of <a href="/wiki/Unite_the_Union" title="Unite the Union">Unite the Union</a>), while other branches of Unite the Union and the <a href="/wiki/GMB_Union" class="mw-redirect" title="GMB Union">GMB Union</a> represent other employees. Bob Ayling\'s management faced strike action by cabin crew over a \xc2\xa31 billion cost-cutting drive to return BA to profitability in 1997; this was the last time BA cabin crew would strike until 2009, although staff morale has reportedly been unstable since that incident.<sup id="cite_ref-CCSA_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CCSA-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> In an effort to increase interaction between management, employees, and the unions, various conferences and workshops have taken place, often with thousands in attendance.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2005, wildcat action was taken by union members over a decision by <a href="/wiki/Gate_Gourmet" title="Gate Gourmet">Gate Gourmet</a> not to renew the contracts of 670 workers and replace them with agency staff; it is estimated that the strike cost British Airways \xc2\xa330&#160;million and caused disruption to 100,000 passengers.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> In October 2006, BA became involved in a <a href="/wiki/British_Airways_cross_controversy" class="mw-redirect" title="British Airways cross controversy">civil rights dispute</a> when a Christian employee was forbidden to wear a necklace bearing the <a href="/wiki/Christian_cross" title="Christian cross">cross</a>, a religious symbol.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> BA\'s practice of forbidding such symbols has been publicly questioned by British politicians such as the former <a href="/wiki/Home_Secretary" title="Home Secretary">Home Secretary</a> <a href="/wiki/John_Reid,_Baron_Reid_of_Cardowan" title="John Reid, Baron Reid of Cardowan">John Reid</a> and the former <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_for_Foreign_and_Commonwealth_Affairs" title="Secretary of State for Foreign and Commonwealth Affairs">Foreign Secretary</a> <a href="/wiki/Jack_Straw" title="Jack Straw">Jack Straw</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Relations have been turbulent between BA and Unite. In 2007, cabin crew threatened strike action over salary changes to be imposed by BA management. The strike was called off at the last minute, British Airways losing \xc2\xa380&#160;million.<sup id="cite_ref-CCSA_87-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CCSA-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> In December 2009, a ballot for strike action over Christmas received a high level of support,<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> action was blocked by a court injunction that deemed the ballot illegal. Negotiations failed to stop strike action in March, BA withdrew perks for strike participants.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> Allegations were made by the <i>Guardian</i> newspaper that BA had consulted outside firms methods to undermine the unions, the story was later withdrawn.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> A strike was announced for May 2010, British Airways again sought an injunction. Members of the <a href="/wiki/Socialist_Workers_Party_(UK)" title="Socialist Workers Party (UK)">Socialist Workers Party</a> disrupted negotiations between BA management and Unite to prevent industrial action.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> Further disruption struck when <a href="/wiki/Derek_Simpson_(trade_unionist)" title="Derek Simpson (trade unionist)">Derek Simpson</a>, a Unite co-leader, was discovered to have leaked details of confidential negotiations online via Twitter.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> Industrial action re-emerged in 2017, this time by BA\'s Mixed Fleet flight attendants, whom were employed on much less favorable pay and terms and conditions compared to previous cabin staff who joined prior to 2010. A ballot for industrial action was distributed to Mixed Fleet crew in November 2016<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> and resulted in an overwhelming yes majority for industrial action.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> Unite described Mixed Fleet crew as on "poverty pay", with many Mixed Fleet flight attendants sleeping in their cars in between shifts because they cannot afford the fuel to drive home, or operating while sick as they cannot afford to call in sick and lose their pay for the shift. Unite also blasted BA of removing staff travel concessions, bonus payments and other benefits to all cabin crew who undertook industrial action, as well as strike-breaking tactics such as wet-leasing aircraft from other airlines and offering financial incentives for cabin crew not to strike.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> The first dates of strikes during Christmas 2016 were cancelled due to pay negotiations.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> Industrial action by Mixed Fleet commenced in January 2017 after rejecting a pay offer.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> Strike action continued throughout 2017 in numerous discontinuous periods, resulting in one of the longest running disputes in aviation history.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> On 31 October 2017, after 85 days of discontinuous industrial action, Mixed Fleet accepted a new pay deal from BA which ended the dispute.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Destinations">Destinations</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_British_Airways_destinations" title="List of British Airways destinations">List of British Airways destinations</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:British_Airways_747-400_at_New_York_JFK_Airport_(Quintin_Soloviev_-_QFS_Aviation).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/British_Airways_747-400_at_New_York_JFK_Airport_%28Quintin_Soloviev_-_QFS_Aviation%29.jpg/220px-British_Airways_747-400_at_New_York_JFK_Airport_%28Quintin_Soloviev_-_QFS_Aviation%29.jpg" 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/British_Airways_747-400_at_New_York_JFK_Airport_%28Quintin_Soloviev_-_QFS_Aviation%29.jpg/440px-British_Airways_747-400_at_New_York_JFK_Airport_%28Quintin_Soloviev_-_QFS_Aviation%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5406" data-file-height="3603" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a Airport.</div></div></div>\n<p>British Airways serves over 160 destinations, including six domestic.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Codeshare_agreements">Codeshare agreements</span></h3>\n<p>British Airways <a href="/wiki/Codeshare_agreement" title="Codeshare agreement">codeshares</a> with the following airlines:<sup id="cite_ref-CAPA_BA_profile_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CAPA_BA_profile-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="div-col columns column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 17em; -webkit-column-width: 17em; column-width: 17em;">\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Aer_Lingus" title="Aer Lingus">Aer Lingus</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/AirBaltic" title="AirBaltic">airBaltic</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/American_Airlines" title="American Airlines">American Airlines</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Bangkok_Airways" title="Bangkok Airways">Bangkok Airways</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Cathay_Pacific" title="Cathay Pacific">Cathay Pacific</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/China_Eastern_Airlines" title="China Eastern Airlines">China Eastern Airlines</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/China_Southern_Airlines" title="China Southern Airlines">China Southern Airlines</a><sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Finnair" title="Finnair">Finnair</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Flybe" title="Flybe">Flybe</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Iberia_(airline)" title="Iberia (airline)">Iberia</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Japan_Airlines" title="Japan Airlines">Japan Airlines</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/LATAM_Brasil" title="LATAM Brasil">LATAM Brasil</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/LATAM_Chile" title="LATAM Chile">LATAM Chile</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Loganair" title="Loganair">Loganair</a><sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Qantas" title="Qantas">Qantas</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Qatar_Airways" title="Qatar Airways">Qatar Airways</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Royal_Jordanian" title="Royal Jordanian">Royal Jordanian</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/S7_Airlines" title="S7 Airlines">S7 Airlines</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/TAAG_Angola_Airlines" title="TAAG Angola Airlines">TAAG Angola Airlines</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Vueling" title="Vueling">Vueling</a></li></ul>\n</div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Alliance">Alliance</span></h3>\n<p>British Airways is a member and one of the founders of <a href="/wiki/Oneworld" title="Oneworld">Oneworld</a>, an airline alliance.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Fleet">Fleet</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:A380_%26_Red_Arrows_-_RIAT_2013.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/A380_%26_Red_Arrows_-_RIAT_2013.jpg/220px-A380_%26_Red_Arrows_-_RIAT_2013.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/A380_%26_Red_Arrows_-_RIAT_2013.jpg/330px-A380_%26_Red_Arrows_-_RIAT_2013.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/A380_%26_Red_Arrows_-_RIAT_2013.jpg/440px-A380_%26_Red_Arrows_-_RIAT_2013.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1365" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:A380_%26_Red_Arrows_-_RIAT_2013.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>G-XLEA, British Airways\' first Airbus A380, accompanied by the <a href="/wiki/Red_Arrows" title="Red Arrows">Red Arrows</a> at 2013 <a href="/wiki/Royal_International_Air_Tattoo" title="Royal International Air Tattoo">Royal International Air Tattoo</a></div></div></div>\n<p>With the exception of the <a href="/wiki/Boeing_707" title="Boeing 707">Boeing 707</a> and early <a href="/wiki/Boeing_747" title="Boeing 747">Boeing 747</a> variants from BOAC, British Airways inherited a mainly UK-built fleet of aircraft when it was formed in 1974. The airline introduced the <a href="/wiki/Boeing_737" title="Boeing 737">Boeing 737</a> and <a href="/wiki/Boeing_757" title="Boeing 757">Boeing 757</a> into the fleet in the 1980s, followed by the <a href="/wiki/Boeing_747-400" title="Boeing 747-400">Boeing 747-400</a>, <a href="/wiki/Boeing_767" title="Boeing 767">Boeing 767</a> and <a href="/wiki/Boeing_777" title="Boeing 777">Boeing 777</a> in the 1990s. BA was the largest operator of Boeing 747-400s, with 57 in its fleet.<sup id="cite_ref-BAP_16-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BAP-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-EOP9_18-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EOP9-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> Prior to the introduction of the 787, when Boeing built an aircraft for British Airways, it was allocated the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Boeing_customer_codes" title="List of Boeing customer codes">customer code</a> <i>36</i>, which appeared in their aircraft designation as a suffix, such as 737\xe2\x80\x93436.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1991, British Airways placed its first order for 777-200 aircraft, ordering another four for fleet expansion in 2007 at a cost of around US$800&#160;million.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> BA\'s first 777s were fitted with <a href="/wiki/General_Electric_GE90" title="General Electric GE90">General Electric GE90</a> engines, but BA switched to <a href="/wiki/Rolls-Royce_Trent_800" title="Rolls-Royce Trent 800">Rolls-Royce Trent 800s</a> for subsequent aircraft.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Later in 2007, BA announced their order of thirty-six new long-haul aircraft, including twelve <a href="/wiki/Airbus_A380" title="Airbus A380">Airbus A380s</a> and twenty-four <a href="/wiki/Boeing_787_Dreamliner" title="Boeing 787 Dreamliner">Boeing 787 Dreamliners</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-baorder_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-baorder-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Rolls-Royce_Trent" title="Rolls-Royce Trent">Rolls-Royce Trent</a> engines were again selected for both orders with Trent 900s powering the A380s and Trent 1000s powering the 787s. The Boeing 787s will replace 14 of British Airways\' Boeing 767 fleet, while the Airbus A380s will replace 20 of BA\'s Boeing 747-400s and will most likely be used to increase capacity on key routes from London Heathrow.\n</p><p>On 1 August 2008, BA announced orders for six Boeing 777-300ERs and options for four more as an interim measure to cover for delays over the deliveries of their 787-8/9s. Of the six that have been ordered, four will be leased and two will be fully acquired by British Airways.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 22 April 2013, IAG confirmed that it had signed a memorandum of understanding to order 18 A350-1000 aircraft for British Airways, with an option for a further 18. The aircraft would replace some of the airline\'s fleet of Boeing 747-400s.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> Options for 18 Boeing 787 aircraft, part of the original contract signed in 2007, have been converted into firm orders for delivery between 2017 and 2021.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 26 June 2013, British Airways took delivery of its first 787s. The aircraft began operations to Toronto on 1 September 2013, and began service to Newark on 1 October 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> BA\'s first A380 was delivered on 4 July 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> It began regular services to Los Angeles on 24 September 2013, followed by Hong Kong on 22 October 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The combined International Airlines Group entity (that BA is now a part of), operates around 400 aircraft, carries over 62&#160;million passengers annually, and serves more than 200 destinations.<sup id="cite_ref-BAIM_28-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BAIM-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Current_fleet">Current fleet</span></h3>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all margin-right:auto;">TBA</div></abbr>\n</td>\n<td>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th>Total\n</th>\n<th>275\n</th>\n<th>69\n</th>\n<th colspan="6">\n</th></tr></tbody></table>\n</center>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cargo_fleet">Cargo fleet</span></h3>\n<p>IAG\'s cargo division, <a href="/wiki/IAG_Cargo" title="IAG Cargo">IAG Cargo</a>, handles cargo operations using capacity on British Airways\' passenger aircraft. IAG reached an agreement with <a href="/wiki/Qatar_Airways" title="Qatar Airways">Qatar Airways</a> in 2014 to operate flights for IAG Cargo using <a href="/wiki/Boeing_777F" class="mw-redirect" title="Boeing 777F">Boeing 777F</a> of <a href="/wiki/Qatar_Airways#Cargo" title="Qatar Airways">Qatar Airways Cargo</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/British_Airways_World_Cargo" title="British Airways World Cargo">British Airways World Cargo</a> was the airline\'s freight division prior to its merger with Iberia Cargo to form IAG Cargo. Aircraft types used by the division between 1974 and 1983 were <a href="/wiki/Vickers_Vanguard" title="Vickers Vanguard">Vickers 953C</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> Boeing 707-300C<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Boeing_747-200F" class="mw-redirect" title="Boeing 747-200F">Boeing 747-200F</a><sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> while the <a href="/wiki/Boeing_747-400F" class="mw-redirect" title="Boeing 747-400F">Boeing 747-400F</a> was operated from the 1990s to 2001 through <a href="/wiki/Atlas_Air" title="Atlas Air">Atlas Air</a> and 2002 to early 2012 by <a href="/wiki/Global_Supply_Systems" title="Global Supply Systems">Global Supply Systems</a>, of these only one of Atlas Air\'s aircraft wore BA livery,<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> the others flew in Atlas and Global Supply\'s own colours. From 2012 until the termination of Global Supply System\'s contract in 2014, three <a href="/wiki/Boeing_747-8F" class="mw-redirect" title="Boeing 747-8F">Boeing 747-8F</a> aircraft were flown for British Airways World Cargo.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"
